<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Badgeman.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Badgeman.jpg/200px-Badgeman.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Badgeman.jpg/300px-Badgeman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Badgeman.jpg/400px-Badgeman.jpg 2x" data-file-width="451" data-file-height="253" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Badgeman.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some conspiracy theorists believe that "badge man" could have fired the fatal shot that killed President Kennedy.</div></div></div>
<p>"Badge man" and "tin hat man" are figures on the grassy knoll who it is alleged can be seen in the <a href="/wiki/Mary_Moorman" title="Mary Moorman">Mary Moorman</a> photo, taken approximately one-sixth of a second after President Kennedy was struck with the fatal head wound. The figures were first discovered by researchers Jack White and Gary Mack and are discussed in a 1988 documentary called <i><a href="/wiki/The_Men_Who_Killed_Kennedy" title="The Men Who Killed Kennedy">The Men Who Killed Kennedy</a></i>, where it is alleged a third figure can also be seen on the grassy knoll, possibly the eyewitness <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Arnold" title="Gordon Arnold">Gordon Arnold</a>. The "badge man" figure — so called as he appears to be wearing a uniform similar to that worn by a policeman, with a badge prominent — helped fuel conspiracy theories linking Dallas Police officers, or someone <a href="/wiki/Police_impersonation" title="Police impersonation">impersonating a police officer</a>, to the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarrs198988_70-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarrs198988-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Black_dog_man">Black dog man</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Black dog man">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Blackdogman.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Blackdogman.PNG/220px-Blackdogman.PNG" decoding="async" width="220" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Blackdogman.PNG/330px-Blackdogman.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/03/Blackdogman.PNG 2x" data-file-width="373" data-file-height="339" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Blackdogman.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Black Dog Man"</div></div></div>
<p>Another "figure" that has been the subject of conspiracy is the so-called "black dog man" figure who can be seen at the corner of a retaining wall in the Willis and Betzner photo of the assassination. In an interview, Marilyn Sitzman told Josiah Thompson that she saw a young black couple who were eating lunch and drinking Cokes on a bench behind the retaining wall and, therefore, it is possible that the "black dog man" figure is actually the black woman and her child.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> If so, the woman has never come forward to identify herself.
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Killing_of_A_President" class="mw-redirect" title="The Killing of A President">The Killing of A President</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Groden" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Groden">Robert Groden</a> argues that the "black dog man" figure can be seen in a pyracantha bush in frame 413 of the Zapruder film. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Select_Committee_on_Assassinations" title="United States House Select Committee on Assassinations">United States House Select Committee on Assassinations</a> concluded that a head of an individual could be seen but that this individual was situated in front of, rather than behind the bushes.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> Bill Miller argues that this individual is actually the eyewitness Emmett Hudson.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conspiracy_theories">Conspiracy theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Conspiracy theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Conspiracy theorists consider four or five groups, alone or in combination, to be the primary suspects in the assassination of Kennedy: the <a href="/wiki/CIA" class="mw-redirect" title="CIA">CIA</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2003xiv_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2003xiv-301">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meagher_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meagher-302">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Military-industrial_complex" class="mw-redirect" title="Military-industrial complex">military-industrial complex</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2003xiv_301-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2003xiv-301">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meagher_302-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meagher-302">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Organized_crime" title="Organized crime">organized crime</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2003xiv_301-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2003xiv-301">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meagher_302-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meagher-302">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kurtz_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurtz-303">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> the government of <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Meagher_302-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meagher-302">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kurtz_303-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurtz-303">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cuban_exile" title="Cuban exile">Cuban exiles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Meagher_302-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meagher-302">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> Other domestic individuals, groups, or organizations implicated in various conspiracy theories include <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_Johnson" class="mw-redirect" title="Lyndon Johnson">Lyndon Johnson</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008203_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008203-304">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meagher_302-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meagher-302">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kurtz_303-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurtz-303">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Meagher_302-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meagher-302">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kurtz_303-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurtz-303">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sam_Giancana" title="Sam Giancana">Sam Giancana</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008203_304-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008203-304">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J._Edgar_Hoover" title="J. Edgar Hoover">J. Edgar Hoover</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Kurtz_303-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurtz-303">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Earl_Warren" title="Earl Warren">Earl Warren</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008203_304-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008203-304">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Meagher_302-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meagher-302">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secret_Service" title="United States Secret Service">United States Secret Service</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Meagher_302-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meagher-302">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kurtz_303-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurtz-303">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/John_Birch_Society" title="John Birch Society">John Birch Society</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Meagher_302-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meagher-302">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kurtz_303-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurtz-303">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> and far-right wealthy Texans.<sup id="cite_ref-Meagher_302-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meagher-302">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> Some other alleged foreign conspirators include <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008203_304-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008203-304">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008203_304-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008203-304">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meagher_302-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meagher-302">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aristotle_Onassis" title="Aristotle Onassis">Aristotle Onassis</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Kurtz_303-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurtz-303">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> the government of <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">South Vietnam</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-O&#39;Leary_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O&#39;Leary-305">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> and international <a href="/wiki/Drug_lord" title="Drug lord">drug lords</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Meagher_302-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meagher-302">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> including a French heroin syndicate.<sup id="cite_ref-O&#39;Leary_305-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O&#39;Leary-305">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Orleans_conspiracy">New Orleans conspiracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: New Orleans conspiracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Trial_of_Clay_Shaw" title="Trial of Clay Shaw">Trial of Clay Shaw</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Ferrie" title="David Ferrie">David Ferrie</a></div>
<p>Soon after the assassination of President Kennedy, Oswald's activities in <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, during the spring and summer of 1963, came under scrutiny. Three days after the assassination, on November 25, 1963, New Orleans attorney <a href="/wiki/Dean_Andrews_Jr." title="Dean Andrews Jr.">Dean Andrews</a> told the FBI that he received a telephone call from a man named <a href="/wiki/Clay_Bertrand" title="Clay Bertrand">Clay Bertrand</a>, on the day of the assassination, asking him to defend Oswald.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> Andrews would later repeat this claim in testimony to the Warren Commission.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciravolo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/86/Ciravolo.jpg/220px-Ciravolo.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/86/Ciravolo.jpg/330px-Ciravolo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/86/Ciravolo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="285" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciravolo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/David_Ferrie" title="David Ferrie">David Ferrie</a> (second from left) with Lee Harvey Oswald (far right) in the New Orleans <a href="/wiki/Civil_Air_Patrol" title="Civil Air Patrol">Civil Air Patrol</a> in 1955.</div></div></div>
<p>Also, in late November 1963, an employee of New Orleans <a href="/wiki/Private_investigator" title="Private investigator">private investigator</a> <a href="/wiki/Guy_Banister" title="Guy Banister">Guy Banister</a> named <a href="/wiki/Jack_Martin_(investigator)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack Martin (investigator)">Jack Martin</a> began making accusations that fellow Banister employee <a href="/wiki/David_Ferrie" title="David Ferrie">David Ferrie</a> was involved in the JFK assassination. Martin told police that Ferrie "was supposed to have been the getaway pilot in the assassination."<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup> He said that Ferrie had outlined plans to kill Kennedy and that Ferrie might have taught Oswald how to use a rifle with a telescopic sight. Martin claimed that Ferrie had known Oswald from their days in the New Orleans Civil Air Patrol, and that he had seen a photograph, at Ferrie's home, of Oswald in a Civil Air Patrol group.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup>  Ferrie denied any association with Oswald.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was later discovered that Ferrie had attended <a href="/wiki/Civil_Air_Patrol" title="Civil Air Patrol">Civil Air Patrol</a> meetings in New Orleans in the 1950s that were also attended by a teenage Lee Harvey Oswald.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated11_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated11-312">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> In 1993, the <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a> television program <i><a href="/wiki/Frontline_(U.S._TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Frontline (U.S. TV series)">Frontline</a></i> obtained a photograph taken in 1955 (eight years before the assassination) showing Oswald and Ferrie at a <a href="/wiki/Civil_Air_Patrol" title="Civil Air Patrol">Civil Air Patrol</a> cookout with other C.A.P. cadets.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated11_312-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated11-312">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> Whether Oswald's and Ferrie's association in the Civil Air Patrol in 1955 is relevant to their later possible association in 1963 is a subject of debate.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated11_312-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated11-312">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Summers,_Anthony_2013_pp._284-285_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summers,_Anthony_2013_pp._284-285-313">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to several witnesses, in 1963, both Ferrie and Banister were working for lawyer <a href="/wiki/G._Wray_Gill" title="G. Wray Gill">G. Wray Gill</a> on behalf of Gill's client, New Orleans Mafia boss <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Marcello" title="Carlos Marcello">Carlos Marcello</a>, in an attempt to block Marcello's deportation to <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> On the afternoon of November 22, 1963 — the day <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> was assassinated and the day Marcello was acquitted in his deportation case — New Orleans private investigator <a href="/wiki/Guy_Banister" title="Guy Banister">Guy Banister</a> and his employee, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Martin_(investigator)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack Martin (investigator)">Jack Martin</a>, were drinking together at a local bar. On their return to Banister's office, the two men got into a heated argument. According to Martin, Banister said something to which Martin replied, "What are you going to do — kill me like you all did Kennedy?". Banister drew his .357 magnum revolver and <a href="/wiki/Pistol_whip" class="mw-redirect" title="Pistol whip">pistol-whipped</a> Martin several times. Martin, badly injured, went by ambulance to <a href="/wiki/Charity_Hospital_(New_Orleans)" title="Charity Hospital (New Orleans)">Charity Hospital</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Earlier, in the spring of 1963, Oswald had written to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> headquarters of the pro-Castro <a href="/wiki/Fair_Play_for_Cuba_Committee" title="Fair Play for Cuba Committee">Fair Play for Cuba Committee</a>, proposing to rent "a small office at my own expense for the purpose of forming a FPCC branch here in New Orleans".<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> As the sole member of the New Orleans chapter of the Fair Play for Cuba Committee, Oswald ordered 1,000 leaflets with the heading, "Hands Off Cuba" from a local printer.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> On August 16, 1963, Oswald passed out Fair Play for Cuba leaflets in front of the <a href="/wiki/International_Trade_Mart" title="International Trade Mart">International Trade Mart</a> in New Orleans.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One of Oswald's leaflets had the address "544 Camp Street" hand-stamped on it, apparently by Oswald himself.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_320-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-320">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> The address was in the "Newman Building", which from October 1961 to February 1962 housed the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Revolutionary_Council" title="Cuban Revolutionary Council">Cuban Revolutionary Council</a>, a militant anti-Castro group.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup> Around the corner but located in the same building, with a different entrance, was the address 531 Lafayette Street — the address of "Guy Banister Associates", the private detective agency run by Guy Banister. Banister's office was involved in anti-Castro and private investigative activities in the New Orleans area. (A CIA file indicated that in September 1960, the CIA had considered "using Guy Banister Associates for the collection of foreign intelligence, but ultimately decided against it".)<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 1970s, the <a href="/wiki/House_Select_Committee_on_Assassinations" class="mw-redirect" title="House Select Committee on Assassinations">House Select Committee on Assassinations</a> (HSCA) investigated the possible relationship of Oswald to Banister's office. While the committee was unable to interview <a href="/wiki/Guy_Banister" title="Guy Banister">Guy Banister</a> (who died in 1964), the committee did interview his brother Ross Banister. Ross "told the committee that his brother had mentioned seeing Oswald hand out Fair Play for Cuba literature on one occasion. Ross theorized that Oswald had used the 544 Camp Street address on his literature to embarrass Guy."<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Guy Banister's secretary, Delphine Roberts, would later tell author <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Summers" title="Anthony Summers">Anthony Summers</a> that she saw Oswald at Banister's office, and that he filled out one of Banister's "agent" application forms. She said, "Oswald came back a number of times. He seemed to be on familiar terms with Banister and with the office."<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> The House Select Committee on Assassinations investigated Roberts's claims and said that "because of contradictions in Roberts' statements to the committee and lack of independent corroboration of many of her statements, the reliability of her statements could not be determined."<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1966, New Orleans <a href="/wiki/District_Attorney" class="mw-redirect" title="District Attorney">District Attorney</a> <a href="/wiki/Jim_Garrison" title="Jim Garrison">Jim Garrison</a> began an investigation into the assassination of President Kennedy. Garrison's investigation led him to conclude that a group of <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">right-wing</a> extremists, including David Ferrie and Guy Banister, were involved with elements of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> (CIA) in a conspiracy to kill Kennedy. Garrison would later claim that the motive for the assassination was anger over Kennedy's attempts to obtain a peace settlement in both Cuba and Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-Jim_Garrison_Interview_329-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jim_Garrison_Interview-329">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> Garrison also came to believe that New Orleans businessman <a href="/wiki/Clay_Shaw" title="Clay Shaw">Clay Shaw</a> was part of the conspiracy and that Clay Shaw used the pseudonym "<a href="/wiki/Clay_Bertrand" title="Clay Bertrand">Clay Bertrand</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> Garrison further believed that Shaw, Banister, and Ferrie conspired to set up Oswald as a patsy in the JFK assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> On March 1, 1967, Garrison arrested and charged Shaw with conspiring to assassinate President Kennedy. On January 29, 1969, Clay Shaw was brought to trial on these charges, and the jury found him not guilty.
</p><p>In 2003, Judyth Vary Baker — whose employment records show that she worked at the <a href="/wiki/Reily_Foods_Company" title="Reily Foods Company">Reily Coffee Company</a> in New Orleans at the same time Oswald did — appeared in an episode of the television documentary series, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Men_Who_Killed_Kennedy" title="The Men Who Killed Kennedy">The Men Who Killed Kennedy</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner,_Nigel_2003_333-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner,_Nigel_2003-333">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup> Baker claimed that in 1963 she was recruited by Dr. Canute Michaelson to work with Dr. <a href="/wiki/Alton_Ochsner" title="Alton Ochsner">Alton Ochsner</a> and Dr. <a href="/wiki/Mary_S._Sherman" title="Mary S. Sherman">Mary Sherman</a> on a clandestine CIA project to develop a biological weapon that could be used to assassinate Fidel Castro. According to Baker, she and Oswald were hired by Reily in the spring of 1963 as a "cover" for the operation.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup> Baker further claimed that she and Oswald began an affair, and that later Oswald told her about <a href="/wiki/Merida,_Yucatan" class="mw-redirect" title="Merida, Yucatan">Merida</a>, Mexico — a city where he suggested they might begin their lives over again.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner,_Nigel_2003_333-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner,_Nigel_2003-333">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup> According to John McAdams, Baker presents a "classic case of pushing the limits of plausibility too far".<sup id="cite_ref-McAdams_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McAdams-336">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup> Others on both sides of the research community have widely dismissed her claims.<sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup> However, other researchers, including James Fetzer, have concluded that Baker's claims are credible.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="CIA_conspiracy">CIA conspiracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: CIA conspiracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/CIA_Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theory" title="CIA Kennedy assassination conspiracy theory">CIA Kennedy assassination conspiracy theory</a></div>
<p>Addressing speculation that Oswald was a CIA agent or had some relationship with the Agency, the Warren Commission stated in 1964 that their investigation "revealed no evidence that Oswald was ever employed [by the] CIA in any capacity."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReport_of_the_President&#39;s_Commission_on_the_Assassination_of_President_John_F._Kennedy,_Appendix_121964659–660_338-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReport_of_the_President&#39;s_Commission_on_the_Assassination_of_President_John_F._Kennedy,_Appendix_121964659–660-338">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup> The House Select Committee on Assassinations reported similarly in 1979 that "there was no indication in Oswald's CIA file that he had ever had contact with the Agency" and concluded that the CIA was not involved in the assassination of Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-HCSA-IC_339-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HCSA-IC-339">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gaeton_Fonzi" title="Gaeton Fonzi">Gaeton Fonzi</a>, an investigator for the House Select Committee on Assassinations, wrote that investigators were pressured not to look into the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Lee_Harvey_Oswald" title="Lee Harvey Oswald">Lee Harvey Oswald</a> and the CIA. He stated that CIA agent <a href="/wiki/David_Atlee_Phillips" title="David Atlee Phillips">David Atlee Phillips</a>, using the pseudonym "Maurice Bishop", was involved with Oswald prior to the Kennedy assassination in connection with anti-Castro Cuban groups.<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1995, former U.S. Army <a href="/wiki/Military_intelligence" title="Military intelligence">Intelligence</a> officer and <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a> executive assistant <a href="/wiki/John_M._Newman" title="John M. Newman">John M. Newman</a> published evidence that both the CIA and FBI deliberately tampered with their files on Lee Harvey Oswald both before and after the assassination. Furthermore, he found that both agencies withheld information that might have alerted authorities in Dallas that Oswald posed a potential threat to the President. Subsequently, Newman expressed a belief that CIA chief of counter-intelligence <a href="/wiki/James_Angleton" class="mw-redirect" title="James Angleton">James Angleton</a> was probably the key figure in the assassination. According to Newman, only Angleton "had the access, the authority, and the diabolically ingenious mind to manage this sophisticated plot." However, Newman surmised that the cover operation was not under James Angleton, but under <a href="/wiki/Allen_Welsh_Dulles" class="mw-redirect" title="Allen Welsh Dulles">Allen Dulles</a> (the former CIA director, and later Warren Commission member, who had been dismissed by Kennedy after the failed <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Pigs_invasion" class="mw-redirect" title="Bay of Pigs invasion">Bay of Pigs invasion</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Newman_341-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newman-341">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1977, the FBI released 40,000 files pertaining to the assassination of Kennedy, including an April 3, 1967 memorandum from Deputy Director <a href="/wiki/Cartha_DeLoach" class="mw-redirect" title="Cartha DeLoach">Cartha DeLoach</a> to Associate Director <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Tolson" title="Clyde Tolson">Clyde Tolson</a> that was written less than a month after President Johnson learned from <a href="/wiki/J._Edgar_Hoover" title="J. Edgar Hoover">J. Edgar Hoover</a> about CIA plots to kill Fidel Castro.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Washington_Post;_December_13,_1977_342-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Washington_Post;_December_13,_1977-342">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kantor_343-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kantor-343">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup> The memorandum reads: "<a href="/wiki/W._Marvin_Watson" title="W. Marvin Watson">Marvin Watson</a> [adviser to President Johnson] called me late last night and stated that the president had told him, in an off moment, that he was now convinced that there was a plot in connection with the [JFK] assassination. Watson stated the president felt that [the] CIA had had something to do with plot."<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarrs1989298_346-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarrs1989298-346">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup> Later, Cartha DeLoach testified to the <a href="/wiki/Church_Committee" title="Church Committee">Church Committee</a> that he "felt this to be sheer speculation".<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shadow_government_conspiracy">Shadow government conspiracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Shadow government conspiracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One conspiracy theory suggests that a secret or <a href="/wiki/Shadow_government_(conspiracy)" title="Shadow government (conspiracy)">shadow government</a> including wealthy industrialists and right-wing politicians ordered the assassination of Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Seattle_Times_350-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Seattle_Times-350">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Dale_Scott" title="Peter Dale Scott">Peter Dale Scott</a> has indicated that Kennedy's death allowed for policy reversals desired by the secret government to escalate the United States' military involvement in Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-Fresia_351-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fresia-351">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military-industrial_complex">Military-industrial complex</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Military-industrial complex">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the farewell speech given by U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> before he left office on January 17, 1961, warned the nation about the power of the military establishment and the arms industry. "In the councils of government, we must guard against the acquisition of unwarranted influence, whether sought or unsought, by the <a href="/wiki/Military-industrial_complex" class="mw-redirect" title="Military-industrial complex">military-industrial complex</a>. The potential for the disastrous rise of misplaced power exists, and will persist."<sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup> Some conspiracy theorists have argued that Kennedy planned to end the involvement of the United States in Vietnam, and was therefore targeted by those who had an interest in sustained military conflict, including the Pentagon and defense contractors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008207_353-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008207-353">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Former <a href="/wiki/Texas_Senator" class="mw-redirect" title="Texas Senator">Texas Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Yarborough" title="Ralph Yarborough">Ralph Yarborough</a> in 1991 stated: "Had Kennedy lived, I think we would have had no Vietnam War, with all of its traumatic and divisive influences in America.  I think we would have escaped that."<sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to author <a href="/wiki/James_W._Douglass" title="James W. Douglass">James W. Douglass</a>, Kennedy was assassinated because he was turning away from the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> and seeking a negotiated peace with the Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> Douglass argued that this "was not the kind of leadership the CIA, the Joint Chiefs of Staff, and the military-industrial complex wanted in the White House."<sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglass2010384_357-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglass2010384-357">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Stone" title="Oliver Stone">Oliver Stone</a>'s film, <i><a href="/wiki/JFK_(film)" title="JFK (film)">JFK</a></i>, explored the possibility that Kennedy was killed by a conspiracy involving the military-industrial complex.<sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/L._Fletcher_Prouty" title="L. Fletcher Prouty">L. Fletcher Prouty</a>, Chief of Special Operations for the Joint Chiefs of Staff under Kennedy, and the person who inspired the character "Mr. X" in Stone's film, wrote that Kennedy's assassination was actually a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d&#39;état">coup d'état</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secret_Service_conspiracy">Secret Service conspiracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Secret Service conspiracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The House Select Committee on Assassinations reported that it investigated "alleged Secret Service complicity in the assassination" and concluded that the Secret Service was not involved.<sup id="cite_ref-HCSA-IC_339-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HCSA-IC-339">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup> However, the HSCA declared that "the Secret Service was deficient in the performance of its duties."<sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup> Among its findings, the HSCA noted: (1) that President Kennedy had not received adequate protection in Dallas, (2) that the Secret Service possessed information that was not properly analyzed, investigated, or used by the Secret Service in connection with the President's trip to Dallas, and (3) that the Secret Service agents in the motorcade were inadequately prepared to protect the President from a sniper.<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup> The HSCA specifically noted:
</p>
<blockquote><p>No actions were taken by the agent in the right front seat of the presidential limousine <a href="/wiki/Roy_Kellerman" title="Roy Kellerman">Roy Kellerman</a> to cover the President with his body, although it would have been consistent with Secret Service procedure for him to have done so. The primary function of the agent was to remain at all times in close proximity to the President in the event of such emergencies.<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Some argue that the lack of Secret Service protection occurred because Kennedy himself had asked that the Secret Service make itself discreet during the Dallas visit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi200729,_38_363-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi200729,_38-363">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup> However, Vince Palamara, who interviewed several Secret Service agents assigned to the Kennedy detail, disputes this. Palamara reports that Secret Service driver Sam Kinney told him that requests — such as removing the bubble top from the limousine in Dallas, not having agents positioned beside the limousine's rear bumper, and reducing the number of Dallas police motorcycle outriders near the limousine's rear bumper — were not made by Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceD_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceD-157">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i>The Echo from Dealey Plaza</i>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Bolden" title="Abraham Bolden">Abraham Bolden</a> — the first African American on the White House Secret Service detail — claimed to have overheard agents say that they would not protect Kennedy from would-be assassins.<sup id="cite_ref-366" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-366">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Questions regarding the forthrightness of the Secret Service increased in the 1990s when the <a href="/wiki/Assassination_Records_Review_Board" class="mw-redirect" title="Assassination Records Review Board">Assassination Records Review Board</a> — which was created when Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/President_John_F._Kennedy_Assassination_Records_Collection_Act_of_1992" title="President John F. Kennedy Assassination Records Collection Act of 1992">JFK Records Act</a> — requested access to Secret Service records. The Review Board was told by the Secret Service that in January 1995, in violation of the JFK Records Act, the Secret Service destroyed protective survey reports that covered JFK's trips from September 24 through November 8, 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-ARRB-ES_367-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ARRB-ES-367">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-368" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-368">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuban_exiles">Cuban exiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Cuban exiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The House Select Committee on Assassinations wrote: "The committee believes, on the basis of the evidence available to it, that anti-Castro Cuban groups, as groups, were not involved in the assassination of President Kennedy, but that the available evidence does not preclude the possibility that individual members may have been involved".<sup id="cite_ref-HCSA-IC_339-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HCSA-IC-339">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the 1959 <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Revolution" title="Cuban Revolution">Cuban Revolution</a> that brought Fidel Castro to power, many Cubans left Cuba to live in the United States. Many of these exiles hoped to overthrow Castro and return to Cuba. Their hopes were dashed with the failed Bay of Pigs Invasion in 1961, and many blamed President Kennedy for the failure.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers2013205_370-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers2013205-370">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The House Select Committee on Assassinations concluded that some militant Cuban exiles might have participated in Kennedy's murder. These exiles worked closely with CIA operatives in violent activities against Castro's Cuba. In 1979, the committee reported:
</p>
<blockquote><p>President Kennedy's popularity among the Cuban exiles had plunged deeply by 1963. Their bitterness is illustrated in a tape recording of a meeting of anti-Castro Cubans and right-wing Americans in the Dallas suburb of Farmer's Branch on October 1, 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-aarclibrary.org_371-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aarclibrary.org-371">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Author <a href="/wiki/Joan_Didion" title="Joan Didion">Joan Didion</a> explored the Miami anti-Castro Cuban theory in her 1987 book <i>Miami</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-372" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-372">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-373" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-373">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup> She discussed Marita Lorenz's testimony regarding Guillermo Novo, a Cuban exile who was involved in shooting a bazooka at the U.N. building from the East River during a speech by <a href="/wiki/Che_Guevara" title="Che Guevara">Che Guevara</a>. Allegedly, Novo was affiliated with Lee Harvey Oswald and Frank Sturgis and carried weapons with them to a hotel in Dallas just prior to the assassination. These claims, though put forth to the House Assassinations Committee by Lorenz, have never been substantiated. <a href="/wiki/Don_DeLillo" title="Don DeLillo">Don DeLillo</a> dramatized the Cuban theory in his 1988 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Libra_(novel)" title="Libra (novel)">Libra</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organized_crime_conspiracy">Organized crime conspiracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Organized crime conspiracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1964, the <a href="/wiki/Warren_Commission" title="Warren Commission">Warren Commission</a> found no evidence linking Ruby's killing of Oswald with any broader conspiracy to assassinate President Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-WCR-C6_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCR-C6-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The Commission concluded that <a href="/wiki/Lee_Harvey_Oswald" title="Lee Harvey Oswald">Lee Harvey Oswald</a> was the only person responsible for assassinating the President, asserting: "Based on its evaluation of the record, the Commission believes that the  evidence does not establish a significant link between Ruby and organized crime. Both State and Federal officials have indicated that Ruby was not affiliated with organized criminal activity."<sup id="cite_ref-374" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-374">&#91;371&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, in 1979, the House Select Committee on Assassinations wrote: "The committee believes, on the basis of the evidence available to it, that the national syndicate of organized crime, as a group, was not involved in the assassination of President Kennedy, but that the available evidence does not preclude the possibility that individual members may have been involved".<sup id="cite_ref-HCSA-IC_339-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HCSA-IC-339">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/G._Robert_Blakey" title="G. Robert Blakey">Robert Blakey</a>, who was chief counsel for the House Select Committee on Assassinations, would later conclude in his book, <i>The Plot to Kill the President</i>, that <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> Mafia boss <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Marcello" title="Carlos Marcello">Carlos Marcello</a> was likely part of a Mafia conspiracy behind the assassination, and that the Mafia had the means and the opportunity required to carry it out.<sup id="cite_ref-375" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-375">&#91;372&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-376" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-376">&#91;373&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a 1993 <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Post">Washington Post</a></i> article, Blakey added: “It is difficult to dispute the underworld pedigree of Jack Ruby, though the Warren Commission did it in 1964. Author <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Posner" title="Gerald Posner">Gerald Posner</a> similarly ignores Ruby's ties to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Civello" title="Joseph Civello">Joseph Civello</a>, the organized crime boss in Dallas. His relationship with Joseph Campisi, the No. 2 man in the mob in Dallas, is even more difficult to ignore. In fact, Campisi and Ruby were close friends; they had dinner together at Campisi's restaurant, the <a href="/wiki/Campisi%27s_Egyptian_Restaurant" title="Campisi&#39;s Egyptian Restaurant">Egyptian Lounge</a>, on the night before the assassination. After Ruby was jailed for killing Oswald, Campisi regularly visited him. The select committee thought Campisi's connection to Marcello was telling; he told us, for example, that every year at Christmas he sent 260 pounds of Italian sausage to Marcello, a sort of Mafia tribute. We also learned that he called New Orleans up to 20 times a day.”<sup id="cite_ref-377" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-377">&#91;374&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Government documents have revealed that some members of the Mafia worked with the Central Intelligence Agency on assassination attempts against <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuban</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-378" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-378">&#91;375&#93;</a></sup> In summer 1960, the CIA recruited ex-FBI agent <a href="/wiki/Robert_Maheu" title="Robert Maheu">Robert Maheu</a> to approach the West Coast representative of the Chicago mob, <a href="/wiki/John_Roselli" title="John Roselli">Johnny Roselli</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-379" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-379">&#91;376&#93;</a></sup> When Maheu contacted Roselli, Maheu hid the fact that he was sent by the CIA, instead portraying himself as an advocate for international corporations. He offered to pay $150,000 to have Castro killed, but Roselli declined any pay. Roselli introduced Maheu to two men he referred to as "Sam Gold" and "Joe". "Sam Gold" was <a href="/wiki/Sam_Giancana" title="Sam Giancana">Sam Giancana</a>; "Joe" was <a href="/wiki/Santo_Trafficante,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Santo Trafficante, Jr.">Santo Trafficante, Jr.</a>, the Tampa, Florida, boss and one of the most powerful mobsters in pre-revolution Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-380" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-380">&#91;377&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglass201034_381-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglass201034-381">&#91;378&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Kessler_(journalist)" title="Glenn Kessler (journalist)">Glenn Kessler</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> explained: "After Fidel Castro led a revolution that toppled a friendly government in 1959, the CIA was desperate to eliminate him. So the agency sought out a partner equally worried about Castro — the Mafia, which had lucrative investments in Cuban casinos."<sup id="cite_ref-Kessler_382-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kessler-382">&#91;379&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his memoir, <i>Bound by Honor</i>, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Bonanno" class="mw-redirect" title="Bill Bonanno">Bill Bonanno</a>, son of New York Mafia boss <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Bonanno" title="Joseph Bonanno">Joseph Bonanno</a>, disclosed that several Mafia families had long-standing ties with the anti-Castro Cubans through the Havana casinos operated by the Mafia before the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Revolution" title="Cuban Revolution">Cuban Revolution</a>. Many Cuban exiles and Mafia bosses disliked President Kennedy, blaming him for the failed Bay of Pigs Invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers2013205-_383-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers2013205--383">&#91;380&#93;</a></sup> They also disliked his brother, then Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kennedy" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Kennedy">Robert Kennedy</a>, who had conducted an unprecedented legal assault on organized crime.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESummers2013224-_384-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESummers2013224--384">&#91;381&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008208_385-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008208-385">&#91;382&#93;</a></sup> This was especially provocative because several Mafia "families" had allegedly worked with JFK's father, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Kennedy" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Kennedy">Joseph Kennedy</a>, to get JFK elected.<sup id="cite_ref-386" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-386">&#91;383&#93;</a></sup> Both the Mafia and the anti-Castro Cubans were experts in assassination — the Cubans having been trained by the CIA.<sup id="cite_ref-Bill_Moyers_1977_387-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bill_Moyers_1977-387">&#91;384&#93;</a></sup> Bonanno reported that he recognized the high degree of involvement of other Mafia families when Jack Ruby killed Oswald, since Bonanno was aware that Ruby was an associate of Chicago mobster <a href="/wiki/Sam_Giancana" title="Sam Giancana">Sam Giancana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-388" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-388">&#91;385&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some conspiracy researchers have alleged a plot involving elements of the Mafia, the CIA, and the anti-Castro Cubans, including Anthony Summers, who stated: "Sometimes people sort of glaze over about the notion that the Mafia and U.S. intelligence and the anti-Castro activists were involved together in the assassination of President Kennedy. In fact, there's no contradiction there. Those three groups were all in bed together at the time and had been for several years in the fight to topple Fidel Castro."<sup id="cite_ref-389" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-389">&#91;386&#93;</a></sup> News reporter <a href="/wiki/Ruben_Castaneda" title="Ruben Castaneda">Ruben Castaneda</a> wrote in 2012: "Based on the evidence, it is likely that JFK was killed by a coalition of anti-Castro Cubans, the Mob, and elements of the CIA."<sup id="cite_ref-390" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-390">&#91;387&#93;</a></sup> In his book, <i>They Killed Our President</i>, former <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a> governor <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Ventura" title="Jesse Ventura">Jesse Ventura</a> concluded: "John F. Kennedy was murdered by a conspiracy involving disgruntled CIA agents, anti-Castro Cubans, and members of the Mafia, all of whom were extremely angry at what they viewed as Kennedy's <a href="/wiki/Appeasement" title="Appeasement">appeasement</a> policies toward Communist Cuba and the Soviet Union."<sup id="cite_ref-391" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-391">&#91;388&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Carlos_Marcello" title="Carlos Marcello">Carlos Marcello</a> allegedly threatened to assassinate the President to short-circuit his younger brother, Attorney General Robert Kennedy, who was leading the administration's anti-Mafia crusade.<sup id="cite_ref-392" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-392">&#91;389&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-393" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-393">&#91;390&#93;</a></sup> Information released in 2006 by the FBI has led some to conclude that Carlos Marcello confessed to his cellmate in Texas, Jack Van Lanningham, an <a href="/wiki/FBI" class="mw-redirect" title="FBI">FBI</a> informant, using a transistor radio that was bugged by the FBI, to having organized Kennedy's assassination, and that the FBI covered up this information that it had in its possession.<sup id="cite_ref-394" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-394">&#91;391&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his book, <i>Contract on America</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-395" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-395">&#91;392&#93;</a></sup> David Scheim provided evidence that Mafia leaders <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Marcello" title="Carlos Marcello">Carlos Marcello</a>, <a href="/wiki/Santo_Trafficante,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Santo Trafficante, Jr.">Santo Trafficante, Jr.</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Hoffa" title="Jimmy Hoffa">Jimmy Hoffa</a> ordered the assassination of President Kennedy. Scheim cited in particular a 25-fold increase in the number of out-of-state telephone calls from Jack Ruby to associates of these crime bosses in the months before the assassination, and to an attempted confession by Jack Ruby while in prison. <a href="/wiki/David_E._Kaiser" title="David E. Kaiser">David E. Kaiser</a> has also suggested mob involvement in his book, <i>The Road to Dallas</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-396" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-396">&#91;393&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Investigative reporter <a href="/wiki/Jack_Anderson_(columnist)" title="Jack Anderson (columnist)">Jack Anderson</a> concluded that Fidel Castro worked with organized crime figures to arrange the JFK assassination. In his book <i>Peace, War, and Politics</i>, Anderson claimed that Mafia member Johnny Roselli gave him extensive details of the plot. Anderson said that although he was never able to independently confirm Roselli's entire story, many of Roselli's details checked out. Anderson said that Oswald may have played a role in the assassination, but that more than one gunman was involved. Johnny Roselli, as previously noted, had worked with the CIA on assassination attempts against Castro.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/History_(U.S._TV_channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="History (U.S. TV channel)">History Channel</a> program <i><a href="/wiki/The_Men_Who_Killed_Kennedy" title="The Men Who Killed Kennedy">The Men Who Killed Kennedy</a></i> presented additional claims of organized crime involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-397" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-397">&#91;394&#93;</a></sup> Christian David was a <a href="/wiki/Corsican_Mafia" class="mw-redirect" title="Corsican Mafia">Corsican Mafia</a> member interviewed in prison. He said that he was offered the assassination contract on President Kennedy, but that he did not accept it. However, he said that he knew the men who did accept the contract. According to David, there were three shooters. He provided the name of one — <a href="/wiki/Lucien_Sarti" title="Lucien Sarti">Lucien Sarti</a>. David said that since the other two shooters were still alive, it would break a code of conduct for him to identify them. When asked what the shooters were wearing, David noted their <i>modus operandi</i> was to dress in costumes such as official uniforms. Much of Christian David's testimony was confirmed by former Corsican member Michelle Nicole, who was part of the <a href="/wiki/Drug_Enforcement_Administration" title="Drug Enforcement Administration">DEA</a> witness protection program.
</p><p>The book <i>Ultimate Sacrifice</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Lamar_Waldron" title="Lamar Waldron">Lamar Waldron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thom_Hartmann" title="Thom Hartmann">Thom Hartmann</a>, attempted to synthesize these theories with new evidence. The authors argued that government officials felt obliged to help the assassins cover up the truth because the assassination conspiracy had direct ties to American government plots to assassinate Castro. Outraged at Robert Kennedy's attack on organized crime, mob leaders had President Kennedy killed to remove Robert from power. A government investigation of the plot was thwarted, the authors allege, because it would have revealed embarrassing evidence of American government involvement with organized crime in plots to kill Castro.<sup id="cite_ref-398" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-398">&#91;395&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lyndon_B._Johnson_conspiracy">Lyndon B. Johnson conspiracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Lyndon B. Johnson conspiracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office,_November_1963.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg/220px-Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg/330px-Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg/440px-Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2374" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office,_November_1963.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Johnson is sworn in on <i>Air Force One</i> by Judge Hughes. Mrs. Kennedy is to the right and Mrs. Johnson to the left.</div></div></div>
<p>A 2003 Gallup poll indicated that nearly 20% of Americans suspected <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> of being involved in the assassination of Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071273_399-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071273-399">&#91;396&#93;</a></sup> Critics of the Warren Commission have accused Johnson of plotting the assassination because he "disliked" the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_family" title="Kennedy family">Kennedys</a> and feared that he would be dropped from the Democratic ticket for the <a href="/wiki/1964_United_States_presidential_election" title="1964 United States presidential election">1964 election.</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008208–209_400-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroderick2008208–209-400">&#91;397&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-401" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-401">&#91;398&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to journalist <a href="/wiki/Max_Holland" title="Max Holland">Max Holland</a>, the first published allegation that Johnson perpetrated the assassination of Kennedy appeared in <a href="/wiki/Penn_Jones,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Penn Jones, Jr.">Penn Jones, Jr.</a>'s book <i>Forgive My Grief</i>, self-published in May 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-History_News_Network;_April_5,_2004_402-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_News_Network;_April_5,_2004-402">&#91;399&#93;</a></sup> In the book, Jones provided excerpts of a letter purported to have been authored by Jack Ruby charging LBJ with the murder of the President.<sup id="cite_ref-History_News_Network;_April_5,_2004_402-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_News_Network;_April_5,_2004-402">&#91;399&#93;</a></sup> With his 1968 book, <i>The Dark Side of Lyndon Baines Johnson</i>, Joachim Joesten is credited by Bugliosi as being the first conspiracy author to accuse Johnson of having a role in the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275_403-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275-403">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup> According to Joesten, Johnson "played the leading part" in a conspiracy that involved "the Dallas oligarchy and ... local branches of the CIA, the FBI, and the Secret Service".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275_403-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275-403">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup> Others who have indicated there was complicity on the part of Johnson include <a href="/wiki/Jim_Marrs" title="Jim Marrs">Jim Marrs</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275_403-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275-403">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup> Ralph D. Thomas,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275_403-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275-403">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup> J. Gary Shaw,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275_403-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275-403">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup> Larry Harris,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275_403-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275-403">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup> Walt Brown,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275_403-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275-403">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup> Noel Twyman,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275_403-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275-403">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Barr_McClellan" title="Barr McClellan">Barr McClellan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275_403-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071275-403">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup> Craig Zirbel,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071276_404-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071276-404">&#91;401&#93;</a></sup> Phillip F. Nelson,<sup id="cite_ref-405" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-405">&#91;402&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Madeleine_Brown" class="mw-redirect" title="Madeleine Brown">Madeleine Brown</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071280_406-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi20071280-406">&#91;403&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The fact that JFK was seriously considering dropping Johnson from the ticket in favor of NC Governor <a href="/wiki/Terry_Sanford" title="Terry Sanford">Terry Sanford</a> should Kennedy run in 1964 has been cited as a possible motive for Johnson's complicity in the assassination. In 1968, Kennedy's personal secretary <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Lincoln" title="Evelyn Lincoln">Evelyn Lincoln</a> wrote in her book, "<i>Kennedy and Johnson</i>" that President Kennedy had told her that Lyndon B. Johnson would be replaced as Vice President of the United States. That conversation took place on November 19, 1963, just three days before the assassination of President Kennedy and was recorded that evening in her diary and reads as follows:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>As Mr. Kennedy sat in the rocker in my office, his head resting on its back he placed his left leg across his right knee. He rocked slightly as he talked. In a slow pensive voice he said to me, 'You know if I am re-elected in sixty-four, I am going to spend more and more time toward making government service an honorable career. I would like to tailor the executive and legislative branches of government so that they can keep up with the tremendous strides and progress being made in other fields.' 'I am going to advocate changing some of the outmoded rules and regulations in the Congress, such as the seniority rule. To do this I will need as a running mate in sixty-four a man who believes as I do.' Mrs. Lincoln went on to write "I was fascinated by this conversation and wrote it down verbatim in my diary. Now I asked, 'Who is your choice as a running-mate?' 'He looked straight ahead, and without hesitating he replied, 'at this time I am thinking about Governor Terry Sanford of North Carolina. But it will not be Lyndon.'<sup id="cite_ref-407" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-407">&#91;404&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>In 2003, researcher Barr McClellan published the book <i>Blood, Money &amp; Power</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-408" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-408">&#91;405&#93;</a></sup> McClellan claims that Johnson, motivated by the fear of being dropped from the Kennedy ticket in 1964 and the need to cover up various scandals, masterminded Kennedy's assassination with the help of his friend, <a href="/wiki/Austin" class="mw-redirect" title="Austin">Austin</a> attorney <a href="/wiki/Edward_A._Clark" title="Edward A. Clark">Edward A. Clark</a>. The book suggests that a smudged partial fingerprint from the sniper's nest likely belonged to Johnson's associate <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_%22Mac%22_Wallace" class="mw-redirect" title="Malcolm &quot;Mac&quot; Wallace">Malcolm "Mac" Wallace</a>, and that Mac Wallace was, therefore, on the sixth floor of the Depository at the time of the shooting. The book further claims that the killing of Kennedy was paid for by oil magnates, including <a href="/wiki/Clint_Murchison,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="Clint Murchison, Sr.">Clint Murchison</a> and <a href="/wiki/H._L._Hunt" title="H. L. Hunt">H. L. Hunt</a>. McClellan states that the assassination of Kennedy allowed the oil depletion allowance to be kept at 27.5 percent. It remained unchanged during the Johnson presidency. According to McClellan, this resulted in a saving of over $100 million to the <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_in_the_United_States" title="Petroleum in the United States">American oil industry</a>. McClellan's book subsequently became the subject of an episode of Nigel Turner's ongoing documentary television series, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Men_Who_Killed_Kennedy" title="The Men Who Killed Kennedy">The Men Who Killed Kennedy</a></i>. The episode, "The Guilty Men", drew angry condemnation from the Johnson family, Johnson's former aides, and former Presidents <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> (who was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Warren_Commission" title="Warren Commission">Warren Commission</a><sup id="cite_ref-409" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-409">&#91;406&#93;</a></sup>) and <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> following its airing on <a href="/wiki/History_(U.S._TV_channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="History (U.S. TV channel)">The History Channel</a>. The History Channel assembled a committee of historians who concluded the accusations in the documentary were without merit, and The History Channel apologized to the Johnson family and agreed not to air the series in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-410" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-410">&#91;407&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Madeleine_Brown" class="mw-redirect" title="Madeleine Brown">Madeleine Brown</a>, who alleged she was the mistress of Johnson, also implicated him in a conspiracy to kill Kennedy. In 1997, Brown said that Johnson, along with H. L. Hunt, had begun planning Kennedy's demise as early as 1960. Brown claimed that by its fruition in 1963, the conspiracy involved dozens of persons, including the leadership of the FBI and the Mafia, as well as prominent politicians and journalists.<sup id="cite_ref-411" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-411">&#91;408&#93;</a></sup> In the documentary <i><a href="/wiki/The_Men_Who_Killed_Kennedy" title="The Men Who Killed Kennedy">The Men Who Killed Kennedy</a></i>, Madeleine Brown and May Newman (an employee of Texas oilman Clint Murchison) both placed FBI Director J. Edgar Hoover at a social gathering at Murchison's mansion the night before the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_412-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-412">&#91;409&#93;</a></sup> Also in attendance, according to Brown, were <a href="/wiki/John_J._McCloy" title="John J. McCloy">John McCloy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_R._Brown" title="George R. Brown">George Brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_L._Thornton" title="Robert L. Thornton">R. L. Thornton</a>, and H. L. Hunt.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown,_Madeleine_D._1997_p._166_413-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown,_Madeleine_D._1997_p._166-413">&#91;410&#93;</a></sup> Madeleine Brown claimed that Johnson arrived at the gathering late in the evening and, in a "grating whisper", told her that the "... Kennedys will never embarrass me again — that's no threat — that's a promise."<sup id="cite_ref-Brown,_Madeleine_D._1997_p._166_413-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown,_Madeleine_D._1997_p._166-413">&#91;410&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-414" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-414">&#91;411&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-415" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-415">&#91;412&#93;</a></sup> In addition, Brown said that on New Year's Eve 1963, she met Johnson at the <a href="/wiki/Driskill_Hotel" title="Driskill Hotel">Driskill Hotel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Austin" class="mw-redirect" title="Austin">Austin</a>, Texas and that he confirmed the conspiracy to kill Kennedy, insisting that "the fat cats of Texas and [U.S.] intelligence" had been responsible.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_412-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-412">&#91;409&#93;</a></sup> Brown reiterated her allegations against Johnson in the 2006 documentary <i>Evidence of Revision</i>. In the same documentary, several other Johnson associates also voiced their suspicions of Johnson.
</p><p>Dr. Charles Crenshaw authored the 1992 book <i>JFK: Conspiracy of Silence</i>, along with conspiracy theorists Jens Hansen and J. Gary Shaw. Crenshaw was a third-year surgical resident on the <a href="/wiki/Trauma_team" title="Trauma team">trauma team</a> at Parkland Hospital that attended to President Kennedy. He also treated Oswald after he was shot by Jack Ruby.<sup id="cite_ref-Altman_416-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altman-416">&#91;413&#93;</a></sup> While attending to Oswald, Crenshaw said that he answered a telephone call from Lyndon Johnson. Crenshaw said that Johnson inquired about Oswald's status, and that Johnson demanded a "death-bed confession from the accused assassin [Oswald]".<sup id="cite_ref-Altman_416-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altman-416">&#91;413&#93;</a></sup> Crenshaw said that he relayed Johnson's message to Dr. Shires, but that Oswald was in no condition to give any statement.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_412-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-412">&#91;409&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-417" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-417">&#91;414&#93;</a></sup> Critics of Crenshaw's allegation state that Johnson was in his limousine at the moment the call would have been made, that no one in his car corroborated that the call was made, and that there is no record of such a call being routed through the White House switchboard.<sup id="cite_ref-McAdams;_Witnesses_418-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McAdams;_Witnesses-418">&#91;415&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBugliosi2007416–420_419-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBugliosi2007416–420-419">&#91;416&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Former CIA agent and Watergate figure E. Howard Hunt accused Johnson (along with several CIA agents whom he named) of complicity in the assassination in his posthumously released autobiography <i><a href="/wiki/American_Spy:_My_Secret_History_in_the_CIA,_Watergate,_and_Beyond" class="mw-redirect" title="American Spy: My Secret History in the CIA, Watergate, and Beyond">American Spy: My Secret History in the CIA, Watergate, and Beyond</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-420" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-420">&#91;417&#93;</a></sup> Referencing that section of the book, <a href="/wiki/Tim_Weiner" title="Tim Weiner">Tim Weiner</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> and Joseph C. Goulden of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Times" title="The Washington Times">The Washington Times</a></i> called into question the sincerity of the charges, and <a href="/wiki/William_F._Buckley,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="William F. Buckley, Jr.">William F. Buckley, Jr.</a>, who wrote the foreword, said material "was clearly ghostwritten".<sup id="cite_ref-Weiner_421-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weiner-421">&#91;418&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goulden_422-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goulden-422">&#91;419&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Buckley,_Jr._423-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buckley,_Jr.-423">&#91;420&#93;</a></sup> Shortly afterwards, an audio-taped "<a href="/wiki/Deathbed_confession" title="Deathbed confession">deathbed confession</a>" in which Hunt claimed knowledge of a conspiracy was released by his sons;<sup id="cite_ref-Hedegaard_234-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hedegaard-234">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> the authenticity of the confession was also met with some skepticism.<sup id="cite_ref-Trahair_230-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trahair-230">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Williams_235-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-235">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Timothy_W._Maier_236-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Timothy_W._Maier-236">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1984, convicted swindler <a href="/wiki/Billie_Sol_Estes" title="Billie Sol Estes">Billie Sol Estes</a> made statements to a <a href="/wiki/Grand_Jury" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand Jury">Grand Jury</a> in Texas indicating that he had "inside knowledge" that implicated Johnson in the death of Kennedy and others.<sup id="cite_ref-424" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-424">&#91;421&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ARRB_425-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ARRB-425">&#91;422&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Michael_L._Kurtz" title="Michael L. Kurtz">Michael L. Kurtz</a> wrote that there is no evidence suggesting that Johnson ordered the assassination of Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurtz1993xxviii_426-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurtz1993xxviii-426">&#91;423&#93;</a></sup> According to Kurtz, Johnson believed Fidel Castro was responsible for the assassination and that Johnson covered up the truth because he feared the possibility that retaliatory measures against Cuba might escalate to nuclear war with the Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurtz1993xxviii_426-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurtz1993xxviii-426">&#91;423&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, biographer <a href="/wiki/Robert_Caro" title="Robert Caro">Robert Caro</a> published his fourth volume on Johnson's career, <i>The Passage of Power</i>, which chronicles Johnson's communications and actions as Vice President, and describes the events leading up to the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-Italie_427-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Italie-427">&#91;424&#93;</a></sup>  Caro wrote that "nothing that I have found in my research" points to involvement by Johnson.<sup id="cite_ref-428" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-428">&#91;425&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="George_H.W._Bush_conspiracy">George H.W. Bush conspiracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: George H.W. Bush conspiracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On November 29, 1963, exactly one week after the assassination, an employee of the FBI wrote in a memo that "Mr. George Bush of the Central Intelligence Agency" was given a briefing on the reaction to the assassination by Cuban exiles living in Miami. Some have alleged that the "George Bush" in this memo is future president <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>, who was appointed head of the CIA by president <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> in 1976, 13 years after the assassination.  During <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush_presidential_campaign,_1988" class="mw-redirect" title="George H. W. Bush presidential campaign, 1988">Bush's presidential campaign in 1988</a>, the memo resurfaced, prompting the CIA to claim that the memo was referring to an employee named George Williams Bush.<sup id="cite_ref-429" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-429">&#91;426&#93;</a></sup>  However, George Williams Bush disputed this suggestion, declaring under oath that "I am not the George Bush of the Central Intelligence Agency referred to in the memorandum."  On the website JFK Facts, author Jefferson Morley writes that any communication by George H.W. Bush with the FBI or CIA in November 1963 does not necessarily demonstrate culpability in the assassination; furthermore, it is unclear whether Bush had any affiliation with the CIA prior to his appointment to head the agency in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-stout_430-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stout-430">&#91;427&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bush biographer <a href="/wiki/Kitty_Kelly" title="Kitty Kelly">Kitty Kelly</a> alleges that Bush was unable to remember his whereabouts on the day of Kennedy's assassination, despite the fact that this information is known.  The day of the assassination, Bush flew to <a href="/wiki/Tyler,_Texas" title="Tyler, Texas">Tyler, Texas</a>, to make an appearance ahead of his upcoming <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_Texas,_1964" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Senate election in Texas, 1964">campaign for the U.S. Senate in 1964</a>, and spoke to the FBI about a local who had threatened Kennedy.  The previous day, Bush had been in Dallas to speak at an oil industry meeting.  Morley has suggested the possibility that Bush's report to the FBI was a cover story, but cautioned that "speculation, however plausible, isn't evidence," and that Kelly is "not the most reliable of sources." <sup id="cite_ref-stout_430-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stout-430">&#91;427&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Supporters of the Bush theory have frequently presented photographic evidence of a man resembling Bush in Dealey Plaza at the time of the assassination.  However, Morley argues this evidence is weak, as no comparative measurements of the two men's facial features has been made.  Bush was already an announced Senate candidate for several months by the time of the assassination and thus had received much press attention, and no eyewitnesses have publicly recalled seeing Bush at the scene, though his opponent, incumbent Senator <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Yarborough" title="Ralph Yarborough">Ralph Yarborough</a>, passed by in the presidential motorcade.<sup id="cite_ref-stout_430-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stout-430">&#91;427&#93;</a></sup>
